The Fourth Argument from the Specific Nature of Existence
The author presents the fourth argument that existence, as a specific nature, must be additional to the Necessary's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' own principle of the specific nature (al-ṭabīʿa al-nawʿiyya) forces them to conclude that existence is additional to the essence of the Necessary.

Core Idea
If existence is a specific nature, its consequences must be uniform — so it must be additional everywhere.

The philosophers accept that existence is a specific nature shared by all existents. They also accept that existence is additional to contingent quiddities. The author now uses the uniformity of a specific nature's consequences to argue that existence must likewise be additional to the Necessary. This argument is an imposition (ilzām) because it targets the philosophers' own commitments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic source passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: the author states this is the fourth argument, an imposition on the philosophers.
2
Understand the principle of specific nature
The philosophers agree that for a specific nature, whatever holds for one individual holds for all others. This is the key premise.
The commentary distinguishes specific nature from generic nature, which does not have this uniformity without a differentia.
3
See how the argument applies to existence
The author applies the principle: existence is a specific nature shared by all existents. Therefore, its logical consequences (lawāzim) must be the same for every existent.
4
Note the conclusion and the parallel example
Since existence is already known to be additional to contingent quiddities, it must also be additional to the Necessary's essence. The same reasoning is used by philosophers to prove hyle for celestial spheres.
This step unifies the argument.
5
Clarify key terms and avoid misunderstanding
The commentary explains that 'validity' (ṣiḥḥa) here means necessity, not possibility. Also, the argument only targets philosophers; Ashʿarites reject rational entailment.
This step deepens your understanding of the argument's scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها
المتفرع على ذواتها بخلاف الوجود الذي هو في الواجب فإنه مستغن عما عداه بالكلية ( الزام للحكماء ) القائلين بأن وجود الواجب عين ذاته، وهو الوجه الرابع من تلك الوجوه إلا أنه إلزامي فإن الحكماء اتفقوا على أن الطبيعة النوعية، يصح على كل فرد منها ما يصح على الآخر فنقول : ( الوجود طبيعة نوعية ) مشتركة بين الوجودات ( فلا تختلف لوازمه فلما ثبت كونه زائداً على ماهيات الممكنات عارضاً لها وجب أن يكون في الواجب كذلك (وبه ) أي بما ذكر من أن الطبيعة النوعية، لا يجوز اختلاف لوازمها بل يصح على كل فرد منها ما يصح على سائرها ( أثبت الحكماء الهيولي للفلكيات ( فإنهم أثبتوها في العناصر بأنها قابلة للانفصال كما ستعرفه، ثم قالوا : الأفلاك وإن لم تكن قابلة للانفصال إلا أن الصورة الجسمية طبيعة نوعية، فلما

قوله : ( فلا تختلف لوازمه) أي لا يختلف ما يلزمه بالنظر إلى ذاته في أفراده بأن يكون مثلاً زائداً في البعض وعيناً في البعض الآخر.
قوله : ( كونه زائدا إلخ ) أي بالنظر إلى ذاته من غير نظر إلى خصوصية فرد منه .
قوله : ( بل يصح إلخ ) لما كان الاختلاف يطلق بمعنى التعدد وبمعنى المخالفة والمباينة التعاقب وبمعنى عدم التشابه أضرب عنه بعد إرجاع الضمير إليه بأن المراد منه هاهنا المعنى الآخير، أي يجب تشابه لوازمها في الأفراد، وهو المعنى بقولنا : يصح على كل فرد منها يصح على الآخر، فقولنا : لوازم الطبيعة النوعية لا تختلف في الأفراد، وقولنا : يصح على كل فرد ما يصح على الآخر، بالنظر إلى طبيعته النوعية وقولنا مقتضى الطبيعة النوعية لا يختلف عددها ،واحد لأن ما يجب للفرد بالنظر إلى نفس الطبيعة يكون لازما ومقتضى لها بالضرورة فلا يوقعك اختلاف العبارات حيث جعل المصنف المبني القول الأول والشارح القول الثاني.
First Observatory - Third Objective: That existence is the same as essence or part of it.
What branches off from their essences, unlike the existence that is in the Necessary, for He is independent of everything entirely. (An imposition upon the philosophers) who say that the existence of the Necessary is identical to His essence. And it is the fourth of those aspects, except that it is an obligatory imposition. For the philosophers have agreed that a specific nature (al-ṭabīʿa al-nawʿiyya)—whatever holds for every individual of it holds for the other. So we say: Existence is a specific nature shared among existences, so its logical consequences do not differ. Thus, since it has been established that it is additional to the quiddities of contingents, being accidental to them, it must be likewise in the Necessary. (And by it) i.e., by what was mentioned, that a specific nature does not allow for difference in its consequences, rather whatever holds for every individual holds for all others—(the philosophers established the hyle for the celestial spheres). For they established it in the elements because they are divisible, as you will learn. Then they said: The celestial spheres, even if they are not divisible, yet the corporeal form is a specific nature, so when…
His saying: (For the philosophers have agreed etc.): As for the Ashʿarites, they do not affirm a rational entailment between things or that one thing necessitates another; rather all is referred to His Essence (Most High) initially.
His saying: (The specific nature): As for the generic nature, because it is not realized in itself, it does not necessitate anything except after the addition of the differentia to it, so its consequences may differ due to the difference of differentiae. Its detail is in the commentary on al-Ishārāt concerning the establishment of the hyle for the celestial spheres.
His saying: (Whatever holds for every individual etc.): Thus it occurs in the commentary on al-Ishārāt by the Imām, on the pattern of their saying: 'It is valid for so-and-so' as in al-Asās, i.e., the word 'for' indicates necessity and obligation, and 'validity' in the sense of affirmation, so it amounts to the meaning of necessity. Hence in the new commentary on al-Tajrīd: 'What is necessary for every individual is what is necessary for the other.' What is meant is what is necessary with respect to the nature itself, cutting off consideration of everything else. For what is necessary for an individual by virtue of its particularity is not necessary for another; rather it may be impossible, and that is clear. The intended meaning of validity is not possibility, such that one might object that the necessary consequence of this premise is that all individuals of existence share in the possibility of addition, whereas the intended meaning is that they share in the addition itself.
His saying: (So its logical consequences do not differ): That is, what is necessary for it with respect to its essence does not differ among its individuals, such that it is, for example, additional in some and identical in others.
His saying: (Its being additional etc.): That is, with respect to its essence, without regard to any particularity of an individual.
His saying: (Rather whatever holds etc.): Since 'difference' is used in the sense of multiplicity, and in the sense of opposition and successive distinctness, and in the sense of dissimilarity, he turned away from it after referring the pronoun to it, meaning the last sense here, i.e., the consequences must be similar in the individuals. And that is what is meant by our saying: 'Whatever holds for every individual holds for the other.' So our saying: 'The consequences of the specific nature do not differ among individuals,' and our saying: 'Whatever holds for every individual holds for the other,' with respect to its specific nature, and our saying: 'The requirement of the specific nature does not differ in number,' are all one. For what is necessary for the individual with respect to the nature itself is necessary and a requirement of it necessarily. So the difference of expressions should not mislead you, where the author made the first statement and the commentator made the second.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument, which is an imposition (ilzām) upon the philosophers who claim that existence is identical to the essence of the Necessary. The argument is based on the principle that a specific nature (ṭabīʿa nawʿiyya) applies uniformly to all its individuals: whatever holds for one individual holds for all. The author argues that existence is a specific nature shared by all existents; therefore, its logical consequences (lawāzim) must be the same for all individuals. Since it is established that existence is additional (zāʾid) to the quiddities of contingents, it must also be additional to the essence of the Necessary. The commentary then explains two key points: (1) The Ashʿarites do not accept rational entailment between things, so this argument is only an imposition on the philosophers. (2) There is a distinction between a specific nature (which necessitates uniform consequences) and a generic nature (which does not, due to the addition of differentiae). The commentary further clarifies that 'validity' (ṣiḥḥa) here means necessity, not possibility, and that the intended meaning is that all individuals share in the addition itself, not just the possibility of addition. Finally, the commentary explains that the differences in phrasing between the author and the commentator are not contradictory but express the same meaning: that the consequences of a specific nature are uniform across individuals.

طبيعة جنسية
لازم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument applies universally, including to Ashʿarite theology.
Correction: The commentary clarifies that this argument is an imposition on philosophers only. Ashʿarites reject rational entailment (luzūm) and maintain that all things are directly from Allah's will.

Key Takeaway
The uniformity of a specific nature's consequences compels the conclusion that existence is additional to all quiddities, including the Necessary's essence.

?
The philosophers' own principle was used to confront them. Are there principles you claim to believe but fail to apply to yourself? What would happen if God held you to your own stated standards?

Objection and Answer: Infinity of Knowledge vs. Power
Objection and answer: God's knowledge and power are both infinite yet one exceeds the other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that God's knowledge exceeds His power is answered by distinguishing between actual infinity of knowledge and potential infinity of power.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is actually infinite; His power is infinitely productive, not all at once.

This passage continues the defense of the argument. A second objection claims that if relative quantity implies finitude, then God's knowledge (more objects) exceeds His power (fewer) yet both are infinite. The answer shows that infinity is not a single concept: knowledge is actually infinite, while power is infinite in capacity, not in simultaneous actuality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first sentence. The objector says: if mere relative quantity (fewness and manyness) entails finitude, then God's objects of knowledge exceed His objects of power even though both are infinite. This would contradict the claim that relative quantity implies finitude.
2
Understand the answer's structure
The answer distinguishes two meanings of infinity: one for objects of knowledge, another for objects of power. Notice the two cases given for objects of power.
The key is that 'infinite' is not used univocally.
3
Examine the two relations of power
First, objects of power with the eternal conceptual relation (ta'alluq ma'nawi azali) are actually infinite. Second, objects with the temporal relation (ta'alluq hadith) are infinite in the sense that power never reaches a limit.
The temporal relation is what brings objects into existence.
4
Reflect on the resolution
The objection fails because it treats 'infinite' as a single concept. The argument about relative quantity only applies when comparing things that are infinite in the same sense. Since knowledge and power are infinite in different ways, the objection is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اكتفى بمجرد الاتصاف بالقلة والكثرة، وادعى أنه يستلزم التناهي نقض أيضاً بمعلومات الله تعالى فإنها زائدة على مقدوراته، مع أن كل واحدة منهما غير متناهية . قوله : ( مع أن كل واحدة منهما غير متناهية أما معلوماته تعالى، فعدم تناهيها ظاهر وأما مقدوراته عز وجل فإن أريد بها متعلقات القدرة بالتعلق المعنوي الأزلي الذي لا يترتب عليه وجود المقدور، بل يمكن القادر من إيجاده وتركه فهي أيضاً غير متناهية بالفعل، وإن أريد بها متعلقاتها بالتعلق الذي يترتب عليه وجود المقدور وهو التعلق الحادث على الأظهر، فمعنى عدم تناهيها أن قدرته تعالى لا تصل إلى حد لا يتجاوزه ولا يتعلق بمقدور آخر بعده، فعدم التناهي في المعلومات بمعنى وفي المقدورات بمعنى آخر كما لا يخفى .
And if one is content with mere qualification by fewness and manyness and claims that it entails finitude, it is also refuted by God's objects of knowledge, for they exceed His objects of power, while each of them is infinite. He said: (While each of them is infinite) as for His objects of knowledge, their infinity is apparent; as for His objects of power—if what is meant by them are the objects of power with the eternal conceptual relation (al-ta'alluq al-ma'nawi al-azali) which does not entail the existence of the object but rather enables the powerful one to bring it into existence or leave it, then they are also actually infinite; but if what is meant by them are the objects of power with the relation that entails the existence of the object—which is the temporal relation (al-ta'alluq al-hadith) according to the more apparent view—then the meaning of their infinity is that His power does not reach a limit beyond which it cannot go or be related to another object of power. So the infinity of objects of knowledge is in one sense, and that of objects of power is in another sense, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection (naqd) to the argument. The objector argues that if mere relative quantity (fewness and manyness) implies finitude, then God's objects of knowledge (ma'lumat) are many in relation to His objects of power (maqdurat), yet both are infinite. The answer distinguishes two meanings of infinity. For objects of knowledge, infinity is actual and straightforward. For objects of power, the meaning depends on the type of relation: if it is the eternal conceptual relation (ta'alluq ma'nawi azali) that does not produce existence, the objects are also actually infinite; if it is the temporal relation (ta'alluq hadith) that produces existence, then infinity means that power has no limit to what it can create—not that all objects exist simultaneously. Thus the objection fails because infinity is not univocal.

تعلق حادث

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'infinite' applies in the same way to both God's knowledge and power.
Correction: The answer shows that infinity is not univocal: knowledge is actually infinite (all objects known at once), while power is infinite in the sense of unlimited capacity (not all objects exist at once).

Checkpoint

B) By distinguishing between actual infinity of knowledge and potential infinity of power.
C) By claiming that power is not infinite.
D) By stating that the objection only applies to created things.

Key Takeaway
The objection is resolved by recognizing that infinity is not a single concept; God's knowledge is actually infinite, while His power is potentially infinite in capacity.

Existence as a State: The First Argument Against the Mu'tazila
Al-Jurjani presents the first argument from state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani argues that existence must be a state and how the first argument refutes those Mu'tazila who deny states.

Core Idea
Existence is a state; denying it leads to an impossible consequence.

Al-Jurjani presents a logical argument against the Mu'tazila who deny that existence is a state. He asserts that if divine power had an effect, that effect must reside in a state. Since the Mu'tazila reject states, this leads to a contradiction. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Note that al-Jurjani first states that existence is a state (حال). Then he presents the argument: if divine power had an effect, it would have to be in a state.
Remember that 'state' here means an attribute that is neither existent nor non-existent.
2
Identify the contradiction
The Mu'tazila deny the existence of states. Therefore, the effect of power cannot be in a state, which makes the initial conditional impossible.
This shows that denying states leads to logical absurdity.
3
Review the source
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to see the precise wording of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالذوات أنفسها ( والوجود حال لأنا نثبته بدليله ثم نقول : لنا في الحال من المعتزلة لو كان للقدرة تأثير لكان ذلك التأثير في الحال لكن تأثير القدرة في الحال مع أنه لا حال عندكم أمر محال ( أو نقول : ( لمن أثبت الحال منهم (الذوات أزلية والأحوال ) التي من جملتها الوجود عندكم ( لا تتعلق بها (القدرة فإن الأحوال كما اعترفتم ليست معلومة ولا مجهولة ولا مقدورة ولا معجوزاً عنها وإذا لم تتعلق القدرة بالذوات ولا بالوجود لم يكن الباري سبحانه موجداً للممكنات ولا قادراً على إيجادها وذلك كفر صريح . قوله: (ليست معلومة إلخ) أي بالذات لعدم استقلالها بالتعقل والوجود . قوله : ( وذلك كفر صريح ) فيه أنه لو تم هذا الوجه لدل على جواز تكفير المعتزلة، أنهم لا يكفرونها ، والجواب أن كون اللازم كفراً صريحاً لا يقتضي أن يكون الملزوم صريحاً، ويجوز تكفيرهم فإن التزام الكفر كفر أو لزومه إذا كان صريحاً .
By the essences themselves, and existence is a state (حال), for we affirm it by its proof, then we say: For us regarding the state (الحال) from the Mu'tazila: If power (قدرة) had an effect, that effect would have to be in the state, but the effect of power in the state—while you have no state—is impossible. Or we say (to those who affirm the state among them): The essences are eternal, and the states (الأحوال)—among which is existence according to you—are not subject to power, for the states, as you admit, are neither known nor unknown, neither capable of being brought into being nor incapable. And if power is not connected to the essences nor to existence, then the Most High Creator would not be the bringer of possibles into existence nor powerful over their origination, and that is explicit unbelief. His saying: (They are not known, etc.), i.e., by essence because of their lack of independence in intellection and existence. His saying: (And that is explicit unbelief) implies that if this argument were sound, it would indicate the permissibility of declaring the Mu'tazila unbelievers—but they do not declare them unbelievers. The response is that for the consequent to be explicit unbelief does not necessitate that the antecedent is explicit, and it is permissible to declare them unbelievers, for committing unbelief is unbelief, or its consequence if it is explicit.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage contains two arguments against the Mu'tazila regarding the status of existence as a 'state' (حال). The passage begins by affirming that existence is a state, then presents the first argument: If divine power had an effect, that effect would have to be in the state (since the effect of power must be something). But the Mu'tazila deny that states exist (they do not consider existence a state), so this leads to an impossibility. The second argument is directed at those Mu'tazila who do affirm states: They claim essences are eternal and that states (including existence) are not subject to divine power. The passage then notes that states, by their own admission, are not knowable or possible objects of power. If power does not attach to essences (because they are eternal) nor to existence (because it is a state not subject to power), then God cannot create possibles, which is explicit unbelief. The gloss 'ليست معلومة' explains that states are not known by themselves because they lack independent intellection and existence. The second gloss discusses whether this argument entails takfir (declaring unbelief) of the Mu'tazila. It notes that even if the consequent (the conclusion) is explicit unbelief, the antecedent (the premise) might not be, so the argument does not force takfir; however, it is permissible because committing unbelief (i.e., adhering to a view that necessarily leads to unbelief) is itself unbelief.

Key Terms
حال
وجود
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the reality of states.
Correction: The argument is not about the reality of states but about the logical consequences of denying them.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against the Mu'tazila who deny the state (حال)?
A) If power had an effect, it would be in the state, but they deny the state.
B) Essences are eternal and states are not subject to power.
C) Existence is a state and therefore cannot be created.
D) The Mu'tazila admit that states are known and unknown.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani's first argument shows that denying the state leads to a logical impossibility, thereby supporting his claim that existence is a state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila denied states to avoid theological complications, but their denial led to a logical impossibility. Is there a truth about Allah's attributes that you find uncomfortable—perhaps about His power or knowledge—that you have subtly denied to maintain your own intellectual comfort? Will you honestly examine your beliefs today, even if it means admitting you were wrong?
?
Al-Jurjani uses pure logic to affirm a reality about existence. How often do you let reason guide your faith, or do you dismiss rational arguments when they challenge your preconceptions? What is one area of your ‘aqeedah where you need to apply careful reasoning rather than blind acceptance? Commit to studying it this week.

The Second Argument and Its Implication for Takfir
Second argument and gloss on takfir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument's structure and how the gloss addresses whether this argument necessitates declaring the Mu'tazila unbelievers.

Core Idea
If power does not affect essences or states, creation becomes impossible—a conclusion that can imply unbelief.

The second argument targets Mu'tazila who do affirm states. It shows that if states are not subject to power, and essences are eternal, then God cannot create. The accompanying gloss clarifies the nuanced relationship between logical consequence and takfir.

Guided Path
1
Follow the second argument
Read the argument: essences are eternal, states (including existence) are not subject to power. Since power doesn't attach to either, creation is impossible.
This argument is directed at those Mu'tazila who affirm states.
2
Examine the gloss on 'not known'
The gloss explains that states are not known by themselves because they lack independent intellection and existence.
3
Analyze the takfir discussion
The gloss then discusses whether the conclusion that God is not the creator is explicit unbelief, and whether that means the Mu'tazila are unbelievers. It explains that the logical consequence being unbelief does not automatically make the premise unbelief, but committing to that premise can be considered unbelief.
This is a nuanced point on takfir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالذوات أنفسها ( والوجود حال لأنا نثبته بدليله ثم نقول : لنا في الحال من المعتزلة لو كان للقدرة تأثير لكان ذلك التأثير في الحال لكن تأثير القدرة في الحال مع أنه لا حال عندكم أمر محال ( أو نقول : ( لمن أثبت الحال منهم (الذوات أزلية والأحوال ) التي من جملتها الوجود عندكم ( لا تتعلق بها (القدرة فإن الأحوال كما اعترفتم ليست معلومة ولا مجهولة ولا مقدورة ولا معجوزاً عنها وإذا لم تتعلق القدرة بالذوات ولا بالوجود لم يكن الباري سبحانه موجداً للممكنات ولا قادراً على إيجادها وذلك كفر صريح . قوله: (ليست معلومة إلخ) أي بالذات لعدم استقلالها بالتعقل والوجود . قوله : ( وذلك كفر صريح ) فيه أنه لو تم هذا الوجه لدل على جواز تكفير المعتزلة، أنهم لا يكفرونها ، والجواب أن كون اللازم كفراً صريحاً لا يقتضي أن يكون الملزوم صريحاً، ويجوز تكفيرهم فإن التزام الكفر كفر أو لزومه إذا كان صريحاً .
By the essences themselves, and existence is a state (حال), for we affirm it by its proof, then we say: For us regarding the state (الحال) from the Mu'tazila: If power (قدرة) had an effect, that effect would have to be in the state, but the effect of power in the state—while you have no state—is impossible. Or we say (to those who affirm the state among them): The essences are eternal, and the states (الأحوال)—among which is existence according to you—are not subject to power, for the states, as you admit, are neither known nor unknown, neither capable of being brought into being nor incapable. And if power is not connected to the essences nor to existence, then the Most High Creator would not be the bringer of possibles into existence nor powerful over their origination, and that is explicit unbelief. His saying: (They are not known, etc.), i.e., by essence because of their lack of independence in intellection and existence. His saying: (And that is explicit unbelief) implies that if this argument were sound, it would indicate the permissibility of declaring the Mu'tazila unbelievers—but they do not declare them unbelievers. The response is that for the consequent to be explicit unbelief does not necessitate that the antecedent is explicit, and it is permissible to declare them unbelievers, for committing unbelief is unbelief, or its consequence if it is explicit.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage contains two arguments against the Mu'tazila regarding the status of existence as a 'state' (حال). The passage begins by affirming that existence is a state, then presents the first argument: If divine power had an effect, that effect would have to be in the state (since the effect of power must be something). But the Mu'tazila deny that states exist (they do not consider existence a state), so this leads to an impossibility. The second argument is directed at those Mu'tazila who do affirm states: They claim essences are eternal and that states (including existence) are not subject to divine power. The passage then notes that states, by their own admission, are not knowable or possible objects of power. If power does not attach to essences (because they are eternal) nor to existence (because it is a state not subject to power), then God cannot create possibles, which is explicit unbelief. The gloss 'ليست معلومة' explains that states are not known by themselves because they lack independent intellection and existence. The second gloss discusses whether this argument entails takfir (declaring unbelief) of the Mu'tazila. It notes that even if the consequent (the conclusion) is explicit unbelief, the antecedent (the premise) might not be, so the argument does not force takfir; however, it is permissible because committing unbelief (i.e., adhering to a view that necessarily leads to unbelief) is itself unbelief.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second argument automatically means Mu'tazila are unbelievers.
Correction: The gloss clarifies that the logical consequence being unbelief does not mean the premise is unbelief, but it is permissible to declare them unbelievers under certain conditions.

Checkpoint
According to the gloss, what is the relationship between the logical conclusion of the second argument and declaring the Mu'tazila unbelievers?
A) The conclusion being explicit unbelief automatically makes the Mu'tazila unbelievers.
B) The conclusion being explicit unbelief does not necessitate that the premise is explicit unbelief, but it is permissible to declare them unbelievers.
C) The Mu'tazila cannot be declared unbelievers because they affirm states.
D) The gloss argues that the Mu'tazila are definitely unbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that denying divine power over states leads to the impossibility of creation, and the gloss clarifies the nuanced takfir implications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage argues that denying God's power over states leads to the conclusion that He is not the Creator. In your own life, do you ever act as if certain 'states' of your existence—like your health, wealth, or status—are outside His control? How does this argument expose the seriousness of even subtle theological lapses?
?
The gloss distinguishes between a view and its logical consequence. Is there a belief you hold that, if followed strictly, would lead to a contradiction with tawhid? Are you willing to examine the implications of your beliefs, even if uncomfortable?
?
The Mu'tazila are not declared unbelievers despite the logical unbelief of their argument. How does this discussion challenge you to balance the gravity of false beliefs with mercy towards those who hold them? Would you extend that same mercy to someone whose opinion you find clearly wrong?

Divine Attributes

The Objection on the Effect of Power and Its Answer
Objection and answer: attribution is non-existent, not the effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that divine power affects only the attribution of existence to the essence, and the core response that attribution is non-existent, along with the dyer analogy that clarifies the distinction.

Core Idea
Attribution is non-existent, so it cannot be the effect; power makes the essence qualified, not creates the relation.

The discussion begins with an objection: if divine power does not directly create the essence (since it is eternal) nor existence (since it is a state), then its effect must be the attribution of existence to the essence. The reply is that this attribution is a non-existent thing (amr `adami), hence cannot be a real effect. To illustrate, the dyer example shows that making something qualified does not require creating the qualification itself. This lesson unpacks this foundational objection and answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Notice the structure: 'la yuqalu... fa innana naqulu'. Identify the objection and the direct response.
2
Understand why attribution is non-existent
The response states that the attribution is 'amr `adami'. Reflect on why a relation is considered non-existent in this context.
Attribution is a relational concept, not a separate entity.
3
Analyze the dyer analogy
The dyer makes a garment qualified by dye without creating the qualification. This shows how power can make the essence qualified without creating a separate entity.
The analogy is central to the response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال تأثير قدرة الله تعالى إنما هو في اتصاف الذات بالوجود، لأنا نقول : ذلك الاتصاف أمر عدمي فلا يكون أثراً للمؤثر، وفيه بحث لأن المراد أن القدرة إنما تجعل الذات متصفة بالوجود لا أنها توجد الاتصاف والفرق بين ألا ترى أن الصباغ يجعل الثوب متصفاً بالصبغ وإن لم يكن موجداً لاتصافه به . قوه (أمر عدمي) إذ لو وجد لكان له اتصاف بالوجود فننقل الكلام إلى اتصاف الاتصاف، ويلزم التسلسل، وما قيل إنه يجوز أن يكون اتصاف الاتصاف أمراً اعتبارياً فمدفوع بأن الاتصاف بالأمر الذي من شأنه الوجود فرع وجود الصفة كما أنه فرع وجود الموصوف على ما بين في محله . قوله : ( والوجود حال إلخ) فيه بحث إذ لا مجال للتحقيق، بل هو إلزامي ولا إلزام أيضاً لأنه إما أن يعترف الخصم بأن الوجود حال أم لا، فعلى الأول لا يصح قوله : مع أنه لا حال عندكم وعلى الثاني لا يصح قوله : لكان ذلك التأثير في الحال فالأولى أن يقال : في إبطال كون التأثير في الوجود، والوجود ليس بموجود كما قيل في إبطال كونه في الاتصاف أن الاتصاف أمر عدمي إلا أنه لا يتم على رأي القائلين بوجود الوجود . قوله : ( لأنا نقول : ذلك الاتصاف أمر عدمي) إذ لو وجد في الأعيان لكان له اتصاف بالوجود فيها فينقل الكلام إلى اتصاف الاتصاف، ويلزم التسلسل وفيه بحث، إذ من الجائز وجود فرد من الاتصاف هو اتصاف الماهية بالوجود دون سائر الأفراد، وسيشير إليه الشارح في بحث الوجود . قوله وفيه بحث قيل : في بحثه بحث فإن قدرة الإيجاد إذا لم تتعلق بالذوات ولا بالوجود لكون الذوات قديمة، والوجود حالاً وكان الاتصاف عدمياً، وكان هو الأثر ليس إلا بم يكن أثر ما موجوداً، وكان الصور المحسوسة صوراً للأمور العدمية المحضة، وهل يقبل العقل أن يكون للأعدام المحضة صور محسوسة وأن تكون الهوية المحسوسة محض المعدومات المجتمعة، وجوابه أن المنفي تعلق قدرة الإيجاد بالذوات على معنى جعلها ذواتاً، وبالوجود على معنى جعله وجوداً، فالمثبت تعلقها بالذوات باعتبار جعلها متصفة بالوجود، فالأثر وهو الذوات بالاعتبار المذكور موجود بلا ريبة فتأمل . إنما تجعل الذات متصفة بالوجود لا أنها توجد الاتصاف والفرق بين ألا ترى أن الصباغ يجعل الثوب متصفاً بالصبغ وإن لم يكن موجداً لاتصافه به .
It cannot be said that the effect of God's power is only in the attribution of the essence to existence, for we say: that attribution is a non-existent thing (أمر عدمي), so it cannot be the effect of the agent. And there is a discussion (بحث) because what is meant is that power only makes the essence qualified by existence, not that it brings the attribution into existence, and the difference is: do you not see that the dyer makes the garment qualified by dye, even though he does not bring the attribution itself into existence? His saying: (A non-existent thing), for if it existed, it would have an attribution to existence, so the discussion would shift to the attribution of attribution, leading to infinite regress. And what is said—that it is permissible for the attribution of attribution to be a mental consideration—is refuted because the attribution to a thing whose nature is existence is a branch of the existence of the attribute, just as it is a branch of the existence of the subject, as established in its place. His saying: (And existence is a state, etc.) involves discussion, for there is no room for investigation; rather it is a persuasive argument (إلزامي), and there is no persuasion either, because either the opponent admits that existence is a state or not. In the first case, his saying 'while you have no state' is unsound; in the second case, his saying 'so that effect would be in the state' is unsound. So it is better to say: in refuting that the effect is in existence, and existence is not existent, just as it was said in refuting that it is in the attribution, that the attribution is non-existent, except that this is not valid according to the view of those who affirm the existence of existence. His saying: (For we say: that attribution is a non-existent thing), for if it existed in external things, it would have an attribution to existence in them, so the discussion would shift to the attribution of attribution, leading to infinite regress. But there is a discussion: it is possible that there exists an individual of attribution which is the attribution of the essence to existence, not other individuals, and the commentator will indicate this in the discussion of existence. His saying: and there is a discussion; it was said: in his discussion there is discussion: for if the power of origination does not attach to essences (because they are eternal) nor to existence (because it is a state), and the attribution is non-existent, and it is the only effect, then no effect would be existent, and sensible forms would be forms of pure non-existent things. Does reason accept that pure non-existents have sensible forms and that the sensible identity is nothing but collected non-existents? Its answer: what is denied is the attachment of the power of origination to essences in the sense of making them essences, and to existence in the sense of making it existence. What is affirmed is its attachment to essences in the sense of making them qualified by existence. Thus the effect—the essences in the mentioned sense—is existent without doubt, so reflect. It only makes the essence qualified by existence, not that it brings the attribution itself into existence, and the difference is: do you not see that the dyer makes the garment qualified by dye, even though he does not bring the attribution into existence?
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair (اعتراض وجواب) regarding the effect of divine power. The objector says: The effect of God's power is only in the attribution (اتصاف) of the essence to existence, not in the essence itself or in existence. The response is that this attribution is a non-existent thing (أمر عدمي), so it cannot be the real effect of an agent. However, there is a discussion (بحث) because the intended meaning is that power makes the essence qualified by existence, not that it brings the attribution into existence as a separate entity. The dyer analogy clarifies: the dyer makes the garment qualified by dye without creating the attribution of dye to garment as a separate existent. The gloss then justifies why the attribution is non-existent: if it were existent, it would itself require an attribution to existence, leading to infinite regress. The objection about the regress being mental is refuted. Further discussion examines whether the argument that existence is a state (حال) is persuasive (إلزامي) or not, concluding it is better to frame it differently. Later glosses debate the possibility that attribution might be a specific type that does not lead to regress. The final point affirms that the true effect is the essence as qualified by existence, which is existent. The passage ends with a repetition of the dyer analogy: power only makes the essence qualified, not that it creates the attribution itself.

عدم
وجود
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attribution (ittisaaf) is a real relational entity that must be created by God's power.
Correction: The attribution is a non-existent thing (amr `adami); it is not a real entity. Power makes the essence qualified, not the attribution itself.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why cannot the attribution be the effect of divine power?
A) Because it is a mental consideration only.
B) Because it is a non-existent thing (amr `adami).
C) Because it is a state (haal).
D) Because it leads to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The attribution of existence to the essence is not a real effect; power makes the essence qualified by existence, analogous to a dyer dying a garment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even the 'attribution' of existence to God's essence is not a real effect, what does that say about your own attempts to define or describe God? Do you ever treat your mental concepts of God as if they were the reality itself?
?
The dyer makes the garment qualified by dye without creating a separate relation. In your worship, do you sometimes focus on the 'relation' (e.g., feeling connected, experiencing emotions) rather than on allowing God's power to actually qualify your soul?
?
This passage distinguishes between making something qualified and creating a relation. Where in your life have you been chasing after effects that are mere shadows (like the attribution) instead of letting the power of God work directly on your essence?

Divine Attributes

Defending the Non-Existent Status of Attribution
Defense of attribution as non-existent via regress and critique of states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the justifications for why the attribution is non-existent—including the infinite regress argument—and the discussion on whether existence is a state, concluding that the true effect is the essence as qualified.

Core Idea
If the attribution were existent, it would require another attribution, leading to regress; the true effect is the essence made qualified.

The previous lesson established that the attribution is non-existent. Now, the text defends this claim by showing that if it were existent, it would lead to an infinite regress (tasalsul). It also addresses the argument that existence is a state (haal) and shows its weakness. Finally, it returns to the dyer analogy to reaffirm that power makes the essence qualified, not the relation.

Guided Path
1
Follow the infinite regress argument
The text argues: if the attribution were existent, it would itself need an attribution to existence, leading to an infinite chain. This is a classic reductio.
2
Refute the mental consideration objection
An opponent may say the regress is only mental; the text replies that attribution to an existent thing is dependent on the existence of the attribute.
3
Examine the state (haal) argument
The text critiques the view that existence is a state, showing that the opponent's earlier argument fails whether they accept states or not.
4
Conclude with the correct effect
The final resolution: the effect is the essence as qualified by existence, which is existent. Power attaches to the essence in the sense of making it qualified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Correction: The effect is the essence as qualified by existence; this is an existent reality.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what would happen if the attribution of the essence to existence were itself existent?
A) It would lead to an infinite regress.
B) It would be a mental consideration.
C) It would be a state.
D) It would be the same as existence.

Key Takeaway
The attribution of existence to the essence cannot be existent, and the true effect of power is the essence as qualified, which is real.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the attribution of existence to your essence is not a separate 'thing' but merely the essence being qualified, how does that change the way you view your own identity? Are you more than just a collection of attributes?
?
The dyer analogy shows that power acts on the essence to make it qualified, not to create the qualification itself. In your life, do you focus on building 'attributes' (like skills or reputation) as if they were independent, while neglecting the transformation of your very essence? What would it mean to let divine power shape your essence directly?
?
The infinite regress argument warns against treating relations as entities. Consider your relationship with God: do you sometimes treat your acts of worship as independent 'things' that need to be qualified by sincerity, rather than letting them flow from a sincerely qualified heart? How can you shift your focus from the act to the state of your heart?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
How Power Makes the Essence Qualified with Existence
Distinction between making essence qualified and creating qualification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between making the essence qualified with an attribute and creating the qualification itself, and how this distinction applies to divine power and existence.

Core Idea
Power qualifies the essence; it does not create the qualification.

This lesson delves into a subtle yet crucial distinction in Islamic theology: when we say that divine power makes an essence existent, it does not mean that power creates the relational link of qualification itself. Rather, power acts on the essence such that existence can be ascribed to it. The Dyer analogy illuminates this abstract point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the opening statement and how the author elaborates on it.
Focus on the difference between making the essence qualified and creating the qualification.
2
Identify the two cases of existence
The passage discusses two interpretations: existence as a real attribute added to the essence, or existence as a mental concept abstracted from the essence. See how the effect of power applies in each case.
In the real case, power acts on the external attribute; in the mental case, it makes the essence a source of effects.
3
Reflect on the dyer analogy
Consider how the dyer making the garment qualified with dye illustrates the broader point. Notice that the dyer does not create the qualification itself.
The analogy bridges the abstract concept to concrete experience.
4
Understand the refutation of the doubt
The author concludes that the effect of power is external, refuting the notion that it is merely mental. Grasp why this is important.
This protects the reality of divine causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنما تجعل الذات متصفة بالوجود لا أنها توجد الاتصاف والفرق بين ألا ترى أن الصباغ يجعل الثوب متصفاً بالصبغ وإن لم يكن موجداً لاتصافه به . قوله : ( إنما تجعل الذات متصفة بالوجود) يعني أن تأثير القدرة في نفس الاتصاف من حيث أنه رابط بين الموصوف والصفة لا من حيث إنها جعلت الاتصاف اتصافاً، ولا من حيث إنها جعلته موجوداً، ثم الاتصاف بالوجود إن كان حقيقياً بأن كان الوجود صفة زائدة على الماهية الخارج، سواء كان موجوداً أو معدوماً فلا إشكال، إذ يكون تأثير القدرة في الأمر الخارجي، وإن كان انتزاعياً فمعنى تأثير القدرة أنها تجعل الذات مصدر الآثار المطلوبة، ومظهر الأحكام المختصة، وهذا هو المراد بقولهم: إنها تجعلها بحيث ينتزع منها الوجود، ثم أثر القدرة هو الذات من حيث الاتصاف، وهو موجود في الخارج فاندفع الشك الذي عرض لبعض الناظرين أنه يلزم أن يكون أثر الفاعل أمراً اعتبارياً وذلك بين البطلان . قوله : ( ألا ترى إلخ) تنوير للمعقول بالمحسوس .
It only makes the essence qualified with existence, not that it creates the qualification. The difference is clear: do you not see that the dyer makes the garment qualified with dye even though he is not the creator of its being qualified with it? His saying: 'It only makes the essence qualified with existence' means that the effect of power (qudra) is on the qualification (ittiṣāf) itself insofar as it is a link between the qualified and the attribute, not insofar as it made the qualification a qualification, nor insofar as it made it existent. Then the qualification with existence, if it is real in that existence is an additional attribute to the essence outside, whether it exists or not, there is no problem, because the effect of power is on the external thing. And if it is mental (iʿtibārī), then the meaning of the effect of power is that it makes the essence a source of desired effects and a manifestation of specific rulings (aḥkām mukhtaṣṣa). This is what is meant by their saying: 'It makes it such that existence is abstracted from it.' Then the effect of power is the essence insofar as it is qualified, and it exists externally. Thus the doubt that occurred to some observers is repelled, that the effect of the agent must be a mental concept (iʿtibārī), and that is clearly false. His saying: 'Do you not see etc.' is an illumination of the rational by the sensory.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of the effect of divine power (qudra) in relation to existence (wujūd). The author explains that when we say power makes the essence qualified with existence, it does not mean that it creates the qualification (ittiṣāf) itself. The qualification is a relational link between the essence and the attribute. Power acts on that relationship by making the essence something from which existence can be abstracted. Two cases are considered: if existence is a real (ḥaqīqī) attribute added to the essence in the external world, then power directly affects that external attribute; if existence is a mental (intizāʿī) concept, then power makes the essence a source of desired effects, allowing existence to be abstracted from it. In both cases, the effect of power is something external, not merely a mental concept. The example of the dyer clarifies the distinction: the dyer makes the garment qualified with dye, but does not create the qualification itself. The author concludes that this resolves the doubt that the effect of power might be purely mental, which is clearly false.

Key Terms
قدرة
اتصاف
وجود
اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The effect of divine power is to create the qualification itself.
Correction: Power makes the essence qualified with existence; it does not create the qualification itself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the dyer example illustrate?
A) That the dyer creates the dye itself.
B) That making something qualified does not require creating the qualification.
C) That the effect of power is always mental.
D) That existence is a real attribute.

Key Takeaway
Divine power makes the essence qualified with existence by affecting the link between essence and attribute, not by creating the qualification itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though your existence is a gift that God's power continuously qualifies you with, or do you take it for granted as a fixed property of yours? What would change if you felt, every moment, that God's power is making you 'qualified with existence' right now?
?
If the effect of divine power is on the link between you and your attributes (like existence), are you conscious that even your very being is being constantly sustained and qualified by God? How does that awareness impact your sense of dependence on Him?
?
The dyer makes the garment qualified with dye without creating the qualification itself. In the same way, God makes you qualified with existence without creating the qualification. Does this distinction affect how you view your own actions and attributes? Are you more aware that your existence is a direct gift from God, or do you sometimes act as if you are the source of your own being?

Intention and the Necessity of Determination
Al-Jurjani presents and answers the second objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection requires possible essences to be determined in non-existence and how the answer refutes it by appealing to God's knowledge.

Core Idea
Intention to create requires distinction, but distinction in God's knowledge suffices.

This lesson examines a key objection against the Ash'ari position that possible entities are not established in non-existence. The objection claims that without pre-existing determination, divine intention to create a specific thing is impossible. We will see how al-Jurjani presents the objection and then gives a concise answer based on God's knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical flow: if possible essences are not determined in non-existence, then intention cannot target a specific one.
The phrase 'ونحو أن' links this to the previous objection.
2
Analyze the logical structure
The objection argues: without determination in non-existence, intention cannot be distinguished; thus no specific intention is possible. Therefore, the essence must be determined and established.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the conclusion
The objection concludes that the possible entity is determined and distinct, hence established in non-existence, which returns to the first argument (the one about 'thubut').
4
Study the answer
The answer is concise: distinction in God's knowledge is sufficient for intention. There is no need for external determination in non-existence.
'والجواب كالجواب' means the answer is the same as before.
5
Reflect on the principle
Consider that God's knowledge individuates all possible things without requiring them to have any reality outside His mind. This preserves divine omnipotence and simplicity.
This opposes the view that intentions require extra-mental distinctness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحو أن القصد إلى إيجاد غير المعين ممتنع فلو لم تكن الذوات الممكنة ثابتة في العدم ومتعينة متميزة فيه لم يتصور من الفاعل القصد إلى إيجادها، فإن ما ليس بمتعين في نفسه لم يتميز القصد إليه عن القصد إلى غيره، فلم يكن هو بكونه مقصوداً بذلك القصد أولى من غيره، ومحصوله أنه متعين متميز، فيكون ثابتاً فقد رجع إلى الوجه الأول، والجواب كالجواب فإن التميز في علم الفاعل كاف في القصد
And similar [to the above] is that intention [قصد] to bring into existence that which is non-specific is impossible. For if the possible essences [ذوات ممكنة] were not established in non-existence and determined and distinct in it, then intention by the agent to bring them into existence would not be conceivable. For what is not determined in itself cannot have intention distinguished from intention to another, so it would not be, by being intended by that intention, more deserving than another. Its conclusion is that it is determined and distinct, so it is established; thus it returns to the first argument. And the answer is like the answer: for distinction [تميز] in the knowledge of the agent is sufficient for intention.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents another objection: If the possible essences were not real and distinct in the state of non-existence, then the agent (God) could not specifically intend to bring one of them into existence, because without determination there is no way to single out one object of intention. Therefore, the possible essences must be determined (مت عين) and distinct (متميز) in non-existence, and hence they are established (ثابت). This reduces to the first argument. The answer, as before (كالجواب), is that distinction in God's knowledge (علم الفاعل) is sufficient for intention; the possible entity need not have external determination to be the object of intention. God's knowledge of it makes it distinct.

Key Terms
القصد
التعين
التميز
علم الفاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intentional causation requires the object to have some form of external reality prior to creation.
Correction: The author argues that God's knowledge alone provides sufficient distinction; no external determination is needed.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is sufficient for God to intend to create a specific possible entity?
A) The entity's existence in external reality.
B) The entity's determination in non-existence.
C) The entity's distinction in God's knowledge.
D) The entity's possibility itself.

Key Takeaway
Distinction in God's knowledge is sufficient for intentional creation; no pre-existing determination is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe Allah knows all things, but do you live as if His knowledge alone is enough for His plans for you? When you feel your situation is undefined or uncertain, do you doubt that Allah can intend good for you? How can you trust more in His knowledge than in visible determination?
?
Consider a good intention you have delayed because you felt the circumstances weren't 'right' or the path wasn't clear. Does this passage challenge that hesitation? If Allah's knowledge suffices for His intention, what does that imply about acting on your own good intentions even when the outcome isn't clear?
?
When you think of your own intentions, do you rely on external signs to confirm them? How would your life change if you believed that your intention, when rooted in sincere desire for Allah's pleasure, is already distinct in His knowledge? Will you commit today to act on a good intention you've been holding back?
